Aluminum was the original correct spelling coined by British Chemist Sir Humphrey Davey in 1812. The spelling was changed in Europe in the 90s because IUPAC wanted to standardise it to match other latin endings from elements on the periodic table. My friends and I recently had this discussion and it turns out that the American spelling is the more accurate one. "1812, coined by English chemist Sir Humphry Davy, from alumina, alumine, the name given by French chemists late 18c. to aluminum oxide, from Latin alumen "alum" (see alum). Davy originally called it alumium (1808), then amended this to aluminum, which remains the U.S. word. British editors in 1812 further amended it to aluminium, the modern preferred British form, to better harmonize with other metallic element names (sodium, potassium, etc.)."
Americans in 2030: Lithum, Sodum, Vanadum, Calcum, Helum

Aluminum was named by British researcher Humphrey Davy in 1808.   Contemporaries outside of Britain (and perhaps inside it as well), did not like the name he gave it despite it being his right to pick the name, and in 1811,  a Swedish researching publishing in French called it aluminium.  This was followed in 1812 by another Brit pissed off about it not sounding classical enough also changed it to aluminium.   So the US/Canada calls it by its proper British name, and everyone else is using something else that makes them feel smarter about it.

AluminiumÂ (aluminumÂ inÂ American and Canadian English) is aÂ chemical elementÂ with theÂ symbol Al andÂ atomic  numberÂ 13. Aluminium has a density lower than those of other commonÂ metals, at approximately one third that ofÂ steel. It has a great affinity towards Â oxygen, andÂ forms a protective layerÂ ofÂ oxideÂ on the surface when exposed to air. Aluminium visually resembles silver, both in its color and in its great ability to reflect light. It is soft,Â non-magneticÂ andÂ ductile. It has one stable isotope,Â 27Al; this isotope is very common, making aluminium the twelfth most common element in the Universe.

Okay so, when the American version of english was made, Merriam Webster wanted 2 things. 1, that American english should be different, distinctly different, even if only slightly, from british english. And 2, spelling should be more streamlined and easier to do.   He removed alot of the fluff. And ever since, Americans, such as myself, have been spelling things more simply, and therefore also pronouncing things more simply. You could call it a more refined and practical version of english. While British english still retains flairs of it's ancient origins.  Therefore, making British English a more fanciful way of saying, or writing things.  Essentially: British English maintains a fancy, or elegant tone. Or maybe you'd say poetic tone.  While American English is streamlined, and simplified. A much easier tone to handle. And therefore theoretically, an easier one to learn, and perhaps write.  If you want to know more, i recommend watching a channel by the name of, "Name Explain." They've got tons of videos on the differences between American English and British English.

So the naming of aluminum/aluminium is a bit messy. Sir Humphrey Davy was the scientist behind the discovery of aluminum, but he couldn't quite settle on a name for a while. Over the course of 4 years he would can it alumium (1808), then alumine (1809), and finally settling on aluminum in 1812.  Someone who wrote a review on one of Davy's lectures referred to it as aluminium .  We call it aluminum in the US and Canada because it was what the actual scientist behind the discovery of the element ultimately wanted it to be called.   The British and most of the rest of the English-speaking world say "aluminium" because some guy misspelled it in a written review. And for some reason it stuck. It wasn't even any of the names Sir Davy ever considered.
â€œAluminumâ€ was the name given to it by the actual person who found it.  â€œAluminiumâ€ was given to it by some anyonymous scientist who thought it sounded cooler. Take this information how you want.

The reason we in the United States spell especially long words like aluminum differently, is that during the beginning era of America, when the best way to get the word out was via newspaper, people were shared for their spots in the paper based off of the amount of letters, so people used simpler vocabulary and removed some "unnecessary" letters like the u in colour and armour. Noah Webster is credited with changing the spelling of so many words. He felt that the U.S.A. being free from Britain needed its own English spelling of words. He believed that English spelling rules were ridiculous, so for his dictionary spelling he replaces words like "colour" with "color", or "theatre" for "theater".  American English spellings by Webster were first printed in his dictionary in 1828 along with many other new American words that didn't exist in Britain at all.  Australia has many similar ideas but they still clung to British spelling for the most part. But they seem be more flexible or accept other spellings. For example Oz prefers -our endings, yet the "Australian Labor Party" does not have the -our spelling in it.  There were some spellings Webster tried to introduce that didn't quite make it. For example "women" would have been "wimmen", "ache" would be "ake", and "sponge" would have been "spunge".

Interesting fact here:  The reason so many words in American English have less letters is because back in the olden days around the 18th to 19th centuries printing companies would charge you based on how many letters there were on the page. To cheese the system American newspapers started dropping â€œunnecessaryâ€ letters. If you read a paper and saw â€œArmorâ€ instead of â€œArmourâ€ you would still understand what the word was.

Fun Fact: The element gets itâ€™s name from alum, a chemical compound the element was discovered in. Itâ€™s name was given to it by English chemist Humphry Davy, who initially called it â€œaluminumâ€ in his 1812 book â€œElements of Chemical Philosophyâ€. However, he was urged to change the name to â€œaluminiumâ€ by his peers in order to conform the elementâ€™s name with the vast majority of elements on the table at the time.    The IUPAC officially recognizes both spellings as correct, but the â€œaluminumâ€ spelling is more common in North America due to the spelling being used by the American Chemical Society since 1925.
